       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Guam" title="Capture of Guam">
        Capture of Guam
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       On June 20, a U.S. fleet commanded by Captain
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_Glass_(admiral)" title="Henry Glass (admiral)">
        Henry Glass
       </a>
       , consisting of the
       <a href="/wiki/Protected_cruiser" title="Protected cruiser">
        protected cruiser
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/USS_Charleston_(C-2)" title="USS Charleston (C-2)">
        USS
        <i>
         Charleston
        </i>
       </a>
       and three transports carrying troops to the Philippines, entered Guam's Apra Harbor, Captain Glass having opened sealed orders instructing him to proceed to
       <a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Guam" title="Capture of Guam">
        Guam and capture it
       </a>
       .
       <i>
        Charleston
       </i>
       fired a few cannon rounds at Fort Santa Cruz without receiving return fire. Two local officials, not knowing that war had been declared and believing the firing had been a salute, came out to
       <i>
        Charleston
       </i>
       to apologize for their inability to return the salute as they were out of gunpowder. Glass informed them that the U.S. and Spain were at war.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208–209_106-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-208–209-106">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The following day, Glass sent Lt. William Braunersruehter to meet the Spanish Governor to arrange the surrender of the island and the Spanish garrison there. Some 54 Spanish infantry were captured and transported to the Philippines as prisoners of war. No U.S. forces were left on Guam, but the only U.S. citizen on the island,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francisco_Portusach_Mart%C3%ADnez" title="Francisco Portusach Martínez">
        Frank Portusach
       </a>
       , told Captain Glass that he would look after things until U.S. forces returned.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208–209_106-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-208–209-106">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Caribbean_theater">
        Caribbean theater
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cuba">
        Cuba
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/San_Juan_Hill_order_of_battle" title="San Juan Hill order of battle">
        San Juan Hill order of battle
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/El_Caney_order_of_battle" title="El Caney order of battle">
        El Caney order of battle
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cristobal-colon_h63229.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="428" data-file-width="740" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Cristobal-colon_h63229.jpg/250px-Cristobal-colon_h63229.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Cristobal-colon_h63229.jpg/375px-Cristobal-colon_h63229.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Cristobal-colon_h63229.jpg/500px-Cristobal-colon_h63229.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cristobal-colon_h63229.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Spanish armored cruiser
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Spanish_cruiser_Crist%C3%B3bal_Col%C3%B3n" title="Spanish cruiser Cristóbal Colón">
           Cristóbal Colón
          </a>
         </i>
         , which was destroyed during the Battle of Santiago on July 3, 1898
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:San_Juan_Hill_by_Kurz_and_Allison.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1383" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/San_Juan_Hill_by_Kurz_and_Allison.JPG/250px-San_Juan_Hill_by_Kurz_and_Allison.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/San_Juan_Hill_by_Kurz_and_Allison.JPG/375px-San_Juan_Hill_by_Kurz_and_Allison.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/San_Juan_Hill_by_Kurz_and_Allison.JPG/500px-San_Juan_Hill_by_Kurz_and_Allison.JPG 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:San_Juan_Hill_by_Kurz_and_Allison.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Detail from
         <i>
          Charge of the
          <a href="/wiki/24th_Infantry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="24th Infantry Regiment (United States)">
           24th
          </a>
          and
          <a href="/wiki/25th_Infantry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="25th Infantry Regiment (United States)">
           25th Colored Infantry
          </a>
          and Rescue of Rough Riders at San Juan Hill, July 2, 1898
         </i>
         depicting the
         <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_San_Juan_Hill" title="Battle of San Juan Hill">
          Battle of San Juan Hill
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">
        Theodore Roosevelt
       </a>
       advocated intervention in Cuba, both for the Cuban people and to promote the
       <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">
        Monroe Doctrine
       </a>
       . While
       <a href="/wiki/Assistant_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="Assistant Secretary of the Navy">
        Assistant Secretary of the Navy
       </a>
       , he placed the Navy on a war-time footing and prepared Dewey's Asiatic Squadron for battle. He also worked with
       <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Wood" title="Leonard Wood">
        Leonard Wood
       </a>
       in convincing the Army to raise an all-volunteer regiment, the 1st U.S. Volunteer Cavalry. Wood was given command of the regiment that quickly became known as the "
       <a href="/wiki/Rough_Riders" title="Rough Riders">
        Rough Riders
       </a>
       ".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
        <a href="#cite_note-107">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Americans planned to capture the city of Santiago de Cuba to destroy Linares' army and Cervera's fleet. To reach Santiago they had to pass through concentrated Spanish defenses in the San Juan Hills and a small town in
       <a href="/wiki/El_Caney" title="El Caney">
        El Caney
       </a>
       . The American forces were aided in Cuba by the pro-independence rebels led by General
       <a href="/wiki/Calixto_Garc%C3%ADa" title="Calixto García">
        Calixto García
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cuban_sentiment">
        Cuban sentiment
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       For quite some time the Cuban public believed the United States government to possibly hold the key to its independence, and even annexation was considered for a time, which historian Louis Pérez explored in his book
       <i>
        Cuba and the United States: Ties of Singular Intimacy
       </i>
       . The Cubans harbored a great deal of discontent towards the Spanish Government, due to years of manipulation on the part of the Spanish. The prospect of getting the United States involved in the fight was considered by many Cubans as a step in the right direction. While the Cubans were wary of the United States' intentions, the overwhelming support from the American public provided the Cubans with some peace of mind, because they believed that the United States was committed to helping them achieve their independence. However, with the imposition of the
       <a href="/wiki/Platt_Amendment" title="Platt Amendment">
        Platt Amendment
       </a>
       of 1903 after the war, as well as economic and military manipulation on the part of the United States, Cuban sentiment towards the United States became polarized, with many Cubans disappointed with continuing American interference.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
        <a href="#cite_note-108">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Land_campaign">
        Land campaign
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       From June 22 to 24, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fifth_Army_Corps_(Spanish-American_War)" title="Fifth Army Corps (Spanish-American War)">
        Fifth Army Corps
       </a>
       under General
       <a href="/wiki/William_Rufus_Shafter" title="William Rufus Shafter">
        William R. Shafter
       </a>
       landed at
       <a href="/wiki/Daiquir%C3%AD" title="Daiquirí">
        Daiquirí
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Siboney,_Cuba" title="Siboney, Cuba">
        Siboney
       </a>
       , east of Santiago, and established an American base of operations. A contingent of Spanish troops, having fought a skirmish with the Americans near Siboney on June 23, had retired to their lightly entrenched positions at
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Las_Guasimas" title="Battle of Las Guasimas">
        Las Guasimas
       </a>
       . An advance guard of U.S. forces under former
       <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_Army" title="Confederate States Army">
        Confederate
       </a>
       General
       <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Wheeler" title="Joseph Wheeler">
        Joseph Wheeler
       </a>
       ignored Cuban scouting parties and orders to proceed with caution. They caught up with and engaged the Spanish rearguard of about 2,000 soldiers led by General
       <a href="/wiki/Antero_Rub%C3%ADn" title="Antero Rubín">
        Antero Rubín
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
        <a href="#cite_note-109">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       who effectively ambushed them, in the Battle of Las Guasimas on June 24. The battle ended indecisively in favor of Spain and the Spanish left Las Guasimas on their planned retreat to Santiago.
      </p>
      <p>
       The U.S. Army employed
       <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">
        Civil War
       </a>
       -era
       <a href="/wiki/Skirmisher#American_Civil_War" title="Skirmisher">
        skirmishers
       </a>
       at the head of the advancing columns. Three of four of the U.S. soldiers who had volunteered to act as skirmishers walking point at the head of the American column were killed, including
       <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Fish_II_(Rough_Rider)" title="Hamilton Fish II (Rough Rider)">
        Hamilton Fish II
       </a>
       (grandson of
       <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Fish" title="Hamilton Fish">
        Hamilton Fish
       </a>
       , the Secretary of State under Ulysses S. Grant), and Captain
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allyn_K._Capron,_Jr." title="Allyn K. Capron, Jr.">
        Allyn K. Capron, Jr.
       </a>
       , whom Theodore Roosevelt would describe as one of the finest natural leaders and soldiers he ever met. Only
       <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_Territory" title="Oklahoma Territory">
        Oklahoma Territory
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Pawnee_people" title="Pawnee people">
        Pawnee
       </a>
       Indian, Tom Isbell, wounded seven times, survived.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roosevelt,_Theodore_p._572_110-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Roosevelt,_Theodore_p._572-110">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Battle of Las Guasimas showed the U.S. that quick-thinking American soldiers would not stick to the linear tactics which did not work effectively against Spanish troops who had learned the art of
       <a href="/wiki/Cover_(military)" title="Cover (military)">
        cover and concealment
       </a>
       from their own struggle with Cuban insurgents, and never made the error of revealing their positions while on the defense. Americans advanced by rushes and stayed in the weeds so that they, too, were largely invisible to the Spaniards who used un-targeted volley fire to try to mass fires against the advancing Americans. While some troops were hit, this technique was mostly a waste of bullets as the Americans learned to duck as soon as they heard the Spanish word Fire, "Fuego" yelled by the Spanish officers. Spanish troops were equipped with smokeless powder arms that also helped them to hide their positions while firing.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Surrender_of_Santiago_news_HD-SN-99-01963.JPEG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2093" data-file-width="3000" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Surrender_of_Santiago_news_HD-SN-99-01963.JPEG/250px-Surrender_of_Santiago_news_HD-SN-99-01963.JPEG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Surrender_of_Santiago_news_HD-SN-99-01963.JPEG/375px-Surrender_of_Santiago_news_HD-SN-99-01963.JPEG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Surrender_of_Santiago_news_HD-SN-99-01963.JPEG/500px-Surrender_of_Santiago_news_HD-SN-99-01963.JPEG 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Surrender_of_Santiago_news_HD-SN-99-01963.JPEG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Receiving the news of the surrender of Santiago
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Regular Spanish troops were mostly armed with modern charger-loaded 1893 7mm Spanish Mauser rifles and using
       <a href="/wiki/Smokeless_powder" title="Smokeless powder">
        smokeless powder
       </a>
       . The high-speed
       <a href="/wiki/7%C3%9757mm_Mauser" title="7×57mm Mauser">
        7×57mm Mauser
       </a>
       round was termed the "Spanish Hornet" by the Americans because of the supersonic crack as it passed overhead. Other irregular troops were armed with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Remington_Rolling_Block" title="Remington Rolling Block">
        Remington Rolling Block
       </a>
       rifles in
       <a href="/wiki/Service_rifle" title="Service rifle">
        .43 Spanish
       </a>
       using smokeless powder and brass-jacketed bullets. US regular infantry were armed with the
       <a href="/wiki/.30-40_Krag" title=".30-40 Krag">
        .30–40
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Krag%E2%80%93J%C3%B8rgensen" title="Krag–Jørgensen">
        Krag–Jørgensen
       </a>
       , a bolt-action rifle with a complex rotating magazine. Both the US regular cavalry and the volunteer cavalry used smokeless ammunition. In later battles, state volunteers used the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Springfield_Model_1873" title="Springfield Model 1873">
        .45–70 Springfield
       </a>
       a single-shot black powder rifle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roosevelt,_Theodore_p._572_110-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Roosevelt,_Theodore_p._572-110">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On July 1, a combined force of about 15,000 American troops in regular infantry and cavalry regiments, including all four of the army's "Colored" regiments, and volunteer regiments, among them Roosevelt and his "
       <a href="/wiki/Rough_Riders" title="Rough Riders">
        Rough Riders
       </a>
       ", the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/71st_Infantry_Regiment_(New_York)" title="71st Infantry Regiment (New York)">
        71st New York
       </a>
       , the 2nd Massachusetts Infantry, and 1st North Carolina, and rebel Cuban forces attacked 1,270 entrenched Spaniards in dangerous Civil War-style frontal assaults at the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_El_Caney" title="Battle of El Caney">
        Battle of El Caney
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_San_Juan_Hill" title="Battle of San Juan Hill">
        Battle of San Juan Hill
       </a>
       outside of Santiago.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
        <a href="#cite_note-111">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       More than 200 U.S. soldiers were killed and close to 1,200 wounded in the fighting, thanks to the high rate of fire the Spanish put down range at the Americans.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
        <a href="#cite_note-112">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Supporting fire by
       <a href="/wiki/Gatling_gun" title="Gatling gun">
        Gatling guns
       </a>
       was critical to the success of the assault.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
        <a href="#cite_note-113">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
        <a href="#cite_note-114">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Cervera decided to escape Santiago two days later. First Lieutenant
       <a href="/wiki/John_J._Pershing" title="John J. Pershing">
        John J. Pershing
       </a>
       , nicknamed "Black Jack", oversaw the 10th Cavalry Unit during the war. Pershing and his unit fought in the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_San_Juan_Hill" title="Battle of San Juan Hill">
        Battle of San Juan Hill
       </a>
       . Pershing was cited for his gallantry during the battle.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Spanish forces at
       <a href="/wiki/Guant%C3%A1namo" title="Guantánamo">
        Guantánamo
       </a>
       were so isolated by Marines and Cuban forces that they did not know that Santiago was under siege, and their forces in the northern part of the province could not break through Cuban lines. This was not true of the Escario relief column from Manzanillo,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
        <a href="#cite_note-115">
         [109]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which fought its way past determined Cuban resistance but arrived too late to participate in the siege.
      </p>
      <p>
       After the battles of San Juan Hill and El Caney, the American advance halted. Spanish troops successfully defended Fort Canosa, allowing them to stabilize their line and bar the entry to Santiago. The Americans and Cubans forcibly began a bloody, strangling siege of the city.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
        <a href="#cite_note-116">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
       During the nights, Cuban troops dug successive series of "trenches" (raised parapets), toward the Spanish positions. Once completed, these parapets were occupied by U.S. soldiers and a new set of excavations went forward. American troops, while suffering daily losses from Spanish fire, suffered far more casualties from
       <a href="/wiki/Hyperthermia" title="Hyperthermia">
        heat exhaustion
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">
        mosquito
       </a>
       -borne disease.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
        <a href="#cite_note-117">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At the western approaches to the city, Cuban general Calixto Garcia began to encroach on the city, causing much panic and fear of reprisals among the Spanish forces.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Naval_operations">
        Naval operations
       </span>
      </h4>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">

          <b>
           does not
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">

           </a>
           any
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            sources
           </a>
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">
           Please help
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Spanish%E2%80%93American_War&amp;action=edit">

         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Santiago_campaign_1898.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="379" data-file-width="1187" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Santiago_campaign_1898.gif/450px-Santiago_campaign_1898.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Santiago_campaign_1898.gif/675px-Santiago_campaign_1898.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Santiago_campaign_1898.gif/900px-Santiago_campaign_1898.gif 2x" width="450"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Santiago_campaign_1898.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Santiago Campaign (1898)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_Battleship_USS_Iowa_BB-4_Crewmen_Pose_1898.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="651" data-file-width="635" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/US_Navy_Battleship_USS_Iowa_BB-4_Crewmen_Pose_1898.jpg/220px-US_Navy_Battleship_USS_Iowa_BB-4_Crewmen_Pose_1898.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/US_Navy_Battleship_USS_Iowa_BB-4_Crewmen_Pose_1898.jpg/330px-US_Navy_Battleship_USS_Iowa_BB-4_Crewmen_Pose_1898.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/US_Navy_Battleship_USS_Iowa_BB-4_Crewmen_Pose_1898.jpg/440px-US_Navy_Battleship_USS_Iowa_BB-4_Crewmen_Pose_1898.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_Battleship_USS_Iowa_BB-4_Crewmen_Pose_1898.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Crewmen pose under the gun turrets of
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/USS_Iowa_(BB-4)" title="USS Iowa (BB-4)">
           Iowa
          </a>
         </i>
         in 1898
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The major port of
       <a href="/wiki/Santiago_de_Cuba" title="Santiago de Cuba">
        Santiago de Cuba
       </a>
       was the main target of naval operations during the war. The U.S. fleet attacking Santiago needed shelter from the summer
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Atlantic_hurricane_seasons" title="List of Atlantic hurricane seasons">
        hurricane season
       </a>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Guant%C3%A1namo_Bay" title="Guantánamo Bay">
        Guantánamo Bay
       </a>
       , with its excellent harbor, was chosen. The
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Guant%C3%A1namo_Bay" title="Battle of Guantánamo Bay">
        1898 invasion of Guantánamo Bay
       </a>
       happened between June 6 and 10, with the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Guant%C3%A1namo_Bay#First_U.S._attack" title="Battle of Guantánamo Bay">
        first U.S. naval attack
       </a>
       and subsequent successful landing of
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">
        U.S. Marines
       </a>
       with naval support.
      </p>
      <p>
       On April 23, a council of senior admirals of the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Navy" title="Spanish Navy">
        Spanish Navy
       </a>
       had decided to order Admiral
       <a href="/wiki/Pascual_Cervera_y_Topete" title="Pascual Cervera y Topete">
        Pascual Cervera y Topete
       </a>
       's squadron of four armored cruisers and three torpedo boat destroyers to proceed from their present location in
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde" title="Cape Verde">
        Cape Verde
       </a>
       (having left from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cadiz,_Spain" title="Cadiz, Spain">
        Cadiz, Spain
       </a>
       ) to the West Indies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
        <a href="#cite_note-118">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Santiago_de_Cuba" title="Battle of Santiago de Cuba">
        Battle of Santiago de Cuba
       </a>
       on July 3, was the largest naval engagement of the Spanish–American War and resulted in the destruction of the Spanish Caribbean Squadron (also known as the
       <i>
        Flota de Ultramar
       </i>
       ). In May, the fleet of Spanish Admiral Pascual Cervera y Topete had been spotted by American forces in Santiago harbor, where they had taken shelter for protection from sea attack. A two-month stand-off between Spanish and American naval forces followed.
      </p>
      <p>
       When the Spanish squadron finally attempted to leave the harbor on July 3, the American forces destroyed or grounded five of the six ships. Only one Spanish vessel, the new armored cruiser
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_cruiser_Crist%C3%B3bal_Col%C3%B3n" title="Spanish cruiser Cristóbal Colón">
        <i>
         Cristóbal Colón
        </i>
       </a>
       , survived, but her captain hauled down her flag and
       <a href="/wiki/Scuttling" title="Scuttling">
        scuttled
       </a>
       her when the Americans finally caught up with her. The 1,612 Spanish sailors who were captured, including Admiral Cervera, were sent to
       <a href="/wiki/Seavey%27s_Island" title="Seavey's Island">
        Seavey's Island
       </a>
       at the
       <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth_Naval_Shipyard" title="Portsmouth Naval Shipyard">
        Portsmouth Naval Shipyard
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Kittery,_Maine" title="Kittery, Maine">
        Kittery, Maine
       </a>
       , where they were confined at
       <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth_Naval_Prison" title="Portsmouth Naval Prison">
        Camp Long
       </a>
       as
       <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_war" title="Prisoner of war">
        prisoners of war
       </a>
       from July 11 until mid-September.
      </p>
      <p>
       During the stand-off, U.S. Assistant Naval Constructor, Lieutenant
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richmond_Pearson_Hobson" title="Richmond Pearson Hobson">
        Richmond Pearson Hobson
       </a>
       had been ordered by Rear Admiral
       <a href="/wiki/William_T._Sampson" title="William T. Sampson">
        William T. Sampson
       </a>
       to sink the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collier_(ship_type)" title="Collier (ship type)">
        collier
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/USS_Merrimac_(1894)" title="USS Merrimac (1894)">
        USS
        <i>
         Merrimac
        </i>
       </a>
       in the harbor to bottle up the Spanish fleet. The mission was a failure, and Hobson and his crew were captured. They were exchanged on July 6, and Hobson became a national hero; he received the
       <a href="/wiki/Medal_of_Honor" title="Medal of Honor">
        Medal of Honor
       </a>
       in 1933, retired as a Rear Admiral and became a Congressman.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._withdrawal">
        U.S. withdrawal
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">
        Yellow fever
       </a>
       had quickly spread amongst the American occupation force, crippling it. A group of concerned officers of the American army chose Theodore Roosevelt to draft
       <a href="/wiki/Round-Robin_Letter_(Spanish%E2%80%93American_War)" title="Round-Robin Letter (Spanish–American War)">
        a request
       </a>
       to Washington that it withdraw the Army, a request that paralleled a similar one from General Shafter, who described his force as an "army of convalescents". By the time of his letter, 75% of the force in Cuba was unfit for service.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vincent_J._Cirillo_2004_119-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Vincent_J._Cirillo_2004-119">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On August 7, the American invasion force started to leave Cuba. The evacuation was not total. The U.S. Army kept the black Ninth US Cavalry Regiment in Cuba to support the occupation. The logic was that their race and the fact that many black volunteers came from southern states would protect them from disease; this logic led to these soldiers being nicknamed "Immunes". Still, when the Ninth left, 73 of its 984 soldiers had contracted the disease.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vincent_J._Cirillo_2004_119-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Vincent_J._Cirillo_2004-119">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Puerto_Rico">
        Puerto Rico
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_Campaign" title="Puerto Rican Campaign">
        Puerto Rican Campaign
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spanish_troops_leaving_Mayagues,_Porto_Rico,_to_engage_the_American_forces_at_Hormiguero,_Puerto_Rico,_08-10-1898_-_NARA_-_533437.tif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2180" data-file-width="2902" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Spanish_troops_leaving_Mayagues%2C_Porto_Rico%2C_to_engage_the_American_forces_at_Hormiguero%2C_Puerto_Rico%2C_08-10-1898_-_NARA_-_533437.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Spanish_troops_leaving_Mayagues%2C_Porto_Rico%2C_to_engage_the_American_forces_at_Hormiguero%2C_Puerto_Rico%2C_08-10-1898_-_NARA_-_533437.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Spanish_troops_leaving_Mayagues%2C_Porto_Rico%2C_to_engage_the_American_forces_at_Hormiguero%2C_Puerto_Rico%2C_08-10-1898_-_NARA_-_533437.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Spanish_troops_leaving_Mayagues%2C_Porto_Rico%2C_to_engage_the_American_forces_at_Hormiguero%2C_Puerto_Rico%2C_08-10-1898_-_NARA_-_533437.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Spanish_troops_leaving_Mayagues%2C_Porto_Rico%2C_to_engage_the_American_forces_at_Hormiguero%2C_Puerto_Rico%2C_08-10-1898_-_NARA_-_533437.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Spanish_troops_leaving_Mayagues%2C_Porto_Rico%2C_to_engage_the_American_forces_at_Hormiguero%2C_Puerto_Rico%2C_08-10-1898_-_NARA_-_533437.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spanish_troops_leaving_Mayagues,_Porto_Rico,_to_engage_the_American_forces_at_Hormiguero,_Puerto_Rico,_08-10-1898_-_NARA_-_533437.tif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Spanish troops before they departed to engage the American forces at
         <a href="/wiki/Hormigueros,_Puerto_Rico" title="Hormigueros, Puerto Rico">
          Hormigueros
         </a>
         , Porto Rico
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In May 1898, Lt. Henry H. Whitney of the United States Fourth Artillery was sent to Puerto Rico on a reconnaissance mission, sponsored by the Army's Bureau of Military Intelligence. He provided maps and information on the Spanish military forces to the U.S. government before the invasion.
      </p>
      <p>
       The American offensive began on May 12, 1898, when a squadron of 12 U.S. ships commanded by Rear Adm.
       <a href="/wiki/William_T._Sampson" title="William T. Sampson">
        William T. Sampson
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">
        United States Navy
       </a>
       attacked the
       <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">
        archipelago
       </a>
       's capital,
       <a href="/wiki/San_Juan,_Puerto_Rico" title="San Juan, Puerto Rico">
        San Juan
       </a>
       . Though the damage inflicted on the city was minimal, the Americans established a
       <a href="/wiki/Blockade" title="Blockade">
        blockade
       </a>
       in the city's harbor,
       <a href="/wiki/San_Juan_Bay" title="San Juan Bay">
        San Juan Bay
       </a>
       . On June 22, the
       <a href="/wiki/Cruiser" title="Cruiser">
        cruiser
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_cruiser_Isabel_II" title="Spanish cruiser Isabel II">
        <i>
         Isabel II
        </i>
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">
        destroyer
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_destroyer_Terror" title="Spanish destroyer Terror">
        <i>
         Terror
        </i>
       </a>
       delivered a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_San_Juan" title="Second Battle of San Juan">
        Spanish counterattack
       </a>
       , but were unable to break the blockade and the
       <i>
        Terror
       </i>
       was damaged.
      </p>
      <p>
       The land offensive began on July 25, when 1,300 infantry soldiers led by
       <a href="/wiki/Nelson_A._Miles" title="Nelson A. Miles">
        Nelson A. Miles
       </a>
       disembarked off the coast of
       <a href="/wiki/Gu%C3%A1nica,_Puerto_Rico" title="Guánica, Puerto Rico">
        Guánica
       </a>
       . The first organized armed opposition occurred in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yauco" title="Yauco">
        Yauco
       </a>
       in what became known as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Yauco" title="Battle of Yauco">
        Battle of Yauco
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAMPR_120-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AAMPR-120">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This encounter was followed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fajardo" title="Battle of Fajardo">
        Battle of Fajardo
       </a>
       . The United States seized control of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fajardo" title="Fajardo">
        Fajardo
       </a>
       on August 1, but were forced to withdraw on August 5 after a group of 200 Puerto Rican-Spanish soldiers led by Pedro del Pino gained control of the city, while most civilian inhabitants fled to a nearby lighthouse. The Americans encountered larger opposition during the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Guayama" title="Battle of Guayama">
        Battle of Guayama
       </a>
       and as they advanced towards the main island's interior. They engaged in crossfire at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Guamani_River_Bridge" title="Battle of the Guamani River Bridge">
        Guamaní River Bridge
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Coamo" title="Battle of Coamo">
        Coamo
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Silva_Heights" title="Battle of Silva Heights">
        Silva Heights
       </a>
       and finally at the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Asomante" title="Battle of Asomante">
        Battle of Asomante
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAMPR_120-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-AAMPR-120">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asomante_121-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Asomante-121">
         [115]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The battles were inconclusive as the allied soldiers retreated.
      </p>
      <p>
       A battle in
       <a href="/wiki/San_Germ%C3%A1n,_Puerto_Rico" title="San Germán, Puerto Rico">
        San Germán
       </a>
       concluded in a similar fashion with the Spanish retreating to
       <a href="/wiki/Lares,_Puerto_Rico" title="Lares, Puerto Rico">
        Lares
       </a>
       . On August 9, 1898, American troops that were pursuing units retreating from Coamo encountered heavy resistance in
       <a href="/wiki/Aibonito,_Puerto_Rico" title="Aibonito, Puerto Rico">
        Aibonito
       </a>
       in a mountain known as
       <i>
        Cerro Gervasio del Asomante
       </i>
       and retreated after six of their soldiers were injured. They returned three days later, reinforced with artillery units and attempted a surprise attack. In the subsequent crossfire, confused soldiers reported seeing Spanish reinforcements nearby and five American officers were gravely injured, which prompted a retreat order. All military actions in Puerto Rico were suspended on August 13, after U.S. President
       <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">
        William McKinley
       </a>
       and French Ambassador
       <a href="/wiki/Jules_Cambon" title="Jules Cambon">
        Jules Cambon
       </a>
       , acting on behalf of the Spanish Government, signed an
       <a href="/wiki/Armistice" title="Armistice">
        armistice
       </a>
       whereby Spain relinquished its sovereignty over Puerto Rico.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asomante_121-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Asomante-121">
         [115]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Making_peace">
        Making peace
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris,_1899.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2256" data-file-width="2752" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris%2C_1899.JPG/250px-Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris%2C_1899.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris%2C_1899.JPG/375px-Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris%2C_1899.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris%2C_1899.JPG/500px-Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris%2C_1899.JPG 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jules_Cambon_signs_Treaty_of_Paris,_1899.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Jules_Cambon" title="Jules Cambon">
          Jules Cambon
         </a>
         , the French Ambassador in the U.S., signing the memorandum of ratification on behalf of Spain
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       With defeats in Cuba and the Philippines, and both of its fleets destroyed, Spain
       <a href="/wiki/Suing_for_peace" title="Suing for peace">
        sued for peace
       </a>
       and negotiations were opened between the two parties. After the sickness and death of British consul Edward Henry Rawson-Walker, American admiral
       <a href="/wiki/George_Dewey" title="George Dewey">
        George Dewey
       </a>
       requested the
       <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">
        Belgian
       </a>
       consul to Manila, Édouard André, to take Rawson-Walker's place as intermediary with the Spanish Government.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
        <a href="#cite_note-122">
         [116]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
        <a href="#cite_note-123">
         [117]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
        <a href="#cite_note-124">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Hostilities were halted on August 12, 1898, with the signing in Washington of a Protocol of Peace between the United States and Spain.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
        <a href="#cite_note-125">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After over two months of
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1898)#Negotiations" title="Treaty of Paris (1898)">
        difficult negotiations
       </a>
       , the formal peace treaty, the
       <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1898)" title="Treaty of Paris (1898)">
        Treaty of Paris
       </a>
       , was signed in Paris on December 10, 1898,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TreatyOfParis_126-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-TreatyOfParis-126">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and was ratified by the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">
        United States Senate
       </a>
       on February 6, 1899.
      </p>
      <p>
       The United States gained all of Spain's colonies outside of
       <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">
        Africa
       </a>
       in the treaty, including the Philippines, Guam and Puerto Rico with the exception of Cuba, which became a U.S.
       <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">
        protectorate
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TreatyOfParis_126-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-TreatyOfParis-126">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The treaty came into force in Cuba April 11, 1899, with Cubans participating only as observers. Having been occupied since July 17, 1898, and thus under the jurisdiction of the United States Military Government (USMG), Cuba formed its own civil government and gained independence on May 20, 1902, with the announced end of USMG jurisdiction over the island. However, the U.S. imposed various restrictions on the new government, including prohibiting alliances with other countries, and reserved the right to intervene. The U.S. also established a perpetual lease of
       <a href="/wiki/Guantanamo_Bay_Naval_Base" title="Guantanamo Bay Naval Base">
        Guantánamo Bay
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">
        Aftermath
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TR_On_Horseback_Back_From_Cuba_1898.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="307" data-file-width="250" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c5/TR_On_Horseback_Back_From_Cuba_1898.jpg/200px-TR_On_Horseback_Back_From_Cuba_1898.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/c/c5/TR_On_Horseback_Back_From_Cuba_1898.jpg 1.5x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TR_On_Horseback_Back_From_Cuba_1898.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         With the end of the war, Colonel
         <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">
          Theodore Roosevelt
         </a>
         musters out of the U.S. Army after the required 30-day quarantine period at
         <a href="/wiki/Montauk,_New_York" title="Montauk, New York">
          Montauk, Long Island
         </a>
         , in 1898.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The war lasted ten weeks.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
        <a href="#cite_note-127">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/John_Hay" title="John Hay">
        John Hay
       </a>
       (the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_the_United_Kingdom" title="United States Ambassador to the United Kingdom">
        United States Ambassador to the United Kingdom
       </a>
       ), writing from London to his friend
       <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">
        Theodore Roosevelt
       </a>
       , declared that it had been "a splendid little war".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
        <a href="#cite_note-128">
         [122]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
        <a href="#cite_note-129">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The press showed
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_United_States" title="Northern United States">
        Northerners
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">
        Southerners
       </a>
       , blacks and whites fighting against a common foe, helping to ease the scars left from the
       <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">
        American Civil War
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
        <a href="#cite_note-130">
         [124]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Exemplary of this was the fact that four former
       <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_Army" title="Confederate States Army">
        Confederate States Army
       </a>
       generals had served in the war, now in the US Army and all of them again carrying similar ranks. These officers included
       <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Butler" title="Matthew Butler">
        Matthew Butler
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fitzhugh_Lee" title="Fitzhugh Lee">
        Fitzhugh Lee
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_L._Rosser" title="Thomas L. Rosser">
        Thomas L. Rosser
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Wheeler" title="Joseph Wheeler">
        Joseph Wheeler
       </a>
       , though only the latter had seen action. Still, in an exciting moment during the Battle of Las Guasimas, Wheeler apparently forgot for a moment which war he was fighting, having supposedly called out "Let's go, boys! We've got the
       <a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">
        damn Yankees
       </a>
       on the run again!"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dupuy794_131-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dupuy794-131">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The war marked
       <a href="/wiki/Foreign_policy_of_the_United_States" title="Foreign policy of the United States">
        American entry
       </a>
       into world affairs. Since then, the U.S. has had a significant hand in various conflicts around the world, and entered many treaties and agreements. The
       <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1893" title="Panic of 1893">
        Panic of 1893
       </a>
       was over by this point, and the U.S. entered a
       <a href="/wiki/American_Century" title="American Century">
        long and prosperous period
       </a>
       of economic and population growth, and technological innovation that lasted through the 1920s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
        <a href="#cite_note-132">
         [126]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The war redefined national identity, served as a solution of sorts to the social divisions plaguing the American mind, and provided a model for all future news reporting.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
        <a href="#cite_note-133">
         [127]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The idea of
       <a href="/wiki/American_imperialism" title="American imperialism">
        American imperialism
       </a>
       changed in the public's mind after the short and successful Spanish–American War. Due to the United States' powerful influence diplomatically and militarily,
       <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">
        Cuba
       </a>
       's status after the war relied heavily upon American actions. Two major developments emerged from the Spanish–American War: one, it greatly enforced the United States' vision of itself as a "defender of democracy" and as a major world power, and two, it had severe implications for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuban%E2%80%93American_relations" title="Cuban–American relations">
        Cuban–American relations
       </a>
       in the future. As historian Louis Pérez argued in his book
       <i>
        Cuba in the American Imagination: Metaphor and the Imperial Ethos
       </i>
       , the Spanish–American War of 1898 "fixed permanently how Americans came to think of themselves: a righteous people given to the service of righteous purpose".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
        <a href="#cite_note-134">
         [128]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The war greatly reduced the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">
        Spanish Empire
       </a>
       . Spain had been declining as an imperial power since the early 19th century as a result of
       <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_War" title="Peninsular War">
        Napoleon's invasion
       </a>
       . The loss of Cuba caused a
       <a href="/wiki/National_trauma" title="National trauma">
        national trauma
       </a>
       because of the affinity of peninsular Spaniards with Cuba, which was seen as another province of Spain rather than as a colony. Spain retained only a handful of overseas holdings:
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_West_Africa" title="Spanish West Africa">
        Spanish West Africa
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Sahara" title="Spanish Sahara">
        Spanish Sahara
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Guinea" title="Spanish Guinea">
        Spanish Guinea
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_Morocco" title="Spanish Morocco">
        Spanish Morocco
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">
        Canary Islands
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The Spanish soldier
       <a href="/wiki/Julio_Cervera_Baviera" title="Julio Cervera Baviera">
        Julio Cervera Baviera
       </a>
       , who served in the
       <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_Campaign" title="Puerto Rican Campaign">
        Puerto Rican Campaign
       </a>
       , published a pamphlet in which he blamed the natives of that colony for its occupation by the Americans, saying, "I have never seen such a servile, ungrateful country [i.e., Puerto Rico].... In twenty-four hours, the people of Puerto Rico went from being fervently Spanish to enthusiastically American.... They humiliated themselves, giving in to the invader as the slave bows to the powerful lord."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
        <a href="#cite_note-135">
         [129]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He was challenged to a
       <a href="/wiki/Duel" title="Duel">
        duel
       </a>
       by a group of young Puerto Ricans for writing this pamphlet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
        <a href="#cite_note-136">
         [130]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Well,_I_hardly_know_which_to_take_first!_5-28-1898.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1175" data-file-width="1536" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Well%2C_I_hardly_know_which_to_take_first%21_5-28-1898.JPG/220px-Well%2C_I_hardly_know_which_to_take_first%21_5-28-1898.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Well%2C_I_hardly_know_which_to_take_first%21_5-28-1898.JPG/330px-Well%2C_I_hardly_know_which_to_take_first%21_5-28-1898.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Well%2C_I_hardly_know_which_to_take_first%21_5-28-1898.JPG/440px-Well%2C_I_hardly_know_which_to_take_first%21_5-28-1898.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Well,_I_hardly_know_which_to_take_first!_5-28-1898.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A cartoon of
         <a href="/wiki/Uncle_Sam" title="Uncle Sam">
          Uncle Sam
         </a>
         seated in restaurant looking at the bill of fare containing "Cuba steak", "Porto Rico pig", the "Philippine Islands" and the "Sandwich Islands" (Hawaii).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Culturally, a new wave called the
       <a href="/wiki/Generation_of_%2798" title="Generation of '98">
        Generation of '98
       </a>
       originated as a response to this trauma, marking a renaissance in Spanish culture. Economically, the war benefited Spain, because after the war large sums of capital held by Spaniards in Cuba and the United States were returned to the peninsula and invested in Spain. This massive flow of capital (equivalent to 25% of the gross domestic product of one year) helped to develop the large modern firms in Spain in the steel, chemical, financial, mechanical, textile, shipyard, and electrical power industries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
        <a href="#cite_note-137">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the political consequences were serious. The defeat in the war began the weakening of the fragile political stability that had been established earlier by the rule of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfonso_XII" title="Alfonso XII">
        Alfonso XII
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The Teller Amendment, which was enacted on April 20, 1898, was a promise from the United States to the Cuban people that it was not declaring war to annex Cuba, but to help it gain its independence from Spain. The
       <a href="/wiki/Platt_Amendment" title="Platt Amendment">
        Platt Amendment
       </a>
       was a move by the United States' government to shape Cuban affairs without violating the Teller Amendment.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
        <a href="#cite_note-138">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Puck_cover2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3461" data-file-width="2466" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Puck_cover2.jpg/200px-Puck_cover2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Puck_cover2.jpg/300px-Puck_cover2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Puck_cover2.jpg/400px-Puck_cover2.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Puck_cover2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The cover of
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Puck_(magazine)" title="Puck (magazine)">
           Puck
          </a>
         </i>
         from April 6, 1901. Caricatures an
         <a href="/wiki/Easter_bonnet" title="Easter bonnet">
          Easter bonnet
         </a>
         made out of a warship that alludes to the gains of the Spanish–American War.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The U.S. Congress had passed the
       <a href="/wiki/Teller_Amendment" title="Teller Amendment">
        Teller Amendment
       </a>
       before the war, promising Cuban independence. However, the Senate passed the Platt Amendment as a rider to an Army
       <a href="/wiki/Appropriations_bill_(United_States)" title="Appropriations bill (United States)">
        appropriations bill
       </a>
       , forcing a peace treaty on Cuba which prohibited it from signing treaties with other nations or contracting a public debt. The Platt Amendment was pushed by imperialists who wanted to project U.S. power abroad (in contrast to the Teller Amendment which was pushed by anti-imperialists who called for a restraint on U.S. rule). The amendment granted the United States the right to stabilize Cuba militarily as needed. In addition, the Platt Amendment permitted the United States to deploy Marines to Cuba if its freedom and independence was ever threatened or jeopardized by an external or internal force. The Platt Amendment also provided for a permanent American naval base in Cuba.
       <a href="/wiki/Guantanamo_Bay_Naval_Base" title="Guantanamo Bay Naval Base">
        Guantánamo Bay
       </a>
       was established after the signing of the
       <a href="/wiki/Cuban%E2%80%93American_Treaty_of_Relations_(1903)" title="Cuban–American Treaty of Relations (1903)">
        Cuban–American Treaty of Relations
       </a>
       in 1903. Thus, despite that Cuba technically gained its independence after the war ended, the United States government ensured that it had some form of power and control over Cuban affairs.
      </p>
      <p>
       The U.S. annexed the former Spanish colonies of Puerto Rico, the Philippines and Guam. The notion of the United States as an imperial power, with colonies, was hotly debated domestically with President McKinley and the Pro-Imperialists winning their way over vocal opposition led by
       <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">
        Democrat
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">
        William Jennings Bryan
       </a>
       , who had supported the war. The American public largely supported the possession of colonies, but there were many outspoken critics such as
       <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">
        Mark Twain
       </a>
       , who wrote
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_War_Prayer" title="The War Prayer">
         The War Prayer
        </a>
       </i>
       in protest.
      </p>
      <p>
       Roosevelt returned to the United States a war hero, and he was soon elected
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_New_York" title="List of Governors of New York">
        governor
       </a>
       of New York and then became the vice president. At the age of 42 he became the youngest man to become President after the assassination of President William McKinley.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="787" data-file-width="1058" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg/250px-The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg/375px-The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg/500px-The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Administration%27s_Promises_Have_Been_Kept.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         1900 Campaign poster
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The war served to further repair relations between the American North and South. The war gave both sides a common enemy for the first time since the end of the Civil War in 1865, and many friendships were formed between soldiers of northern and southern states during their tours of duty. This was an important development, since many soldiers in this war were the children of Civil War veterans on both sides.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
        <a href="#cite_note-139">
         [133]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BuffaloSoldiers-SpanAmWar.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="757" data-file-width="1215" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/BuffaloSoldiers-SpanAmWar.jpg/250px-BuffaloSoldiers-SpanAmWar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/BuffaloSoldiers-SpanAmWar.jpg/375px-BuffaloSoldiers-SpanAmWar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/BuffaloSoldiers-SpanAmWar.jpg/500px-BuffaloSoldiers-SpanAmWar.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BuffaloSoldiers-SpanAmWar.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Segregation in the U.S. military, 1898
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The African-American community strongly supported the rebels in Cuba, supported entry into the war, and gained prestige from their wartime performance in the Army. Spokesmen noted that 33 African-American seamen had died in the
       <i>
        Maine
       </i>
       explosion. The most influential Black leader,
       <a href="/wiki/Booker_T._Washington" title="Booker T. Washington">
        Booker T. Washington
       </a>
       , argued that his race was ready to fight. War offered them a chance "to render service to our country that no other race can", because, unlike Whites, they were "accustomed" to the "peculiar and dangerous climate" of Cuba. One of the Black units that served in the war was the
       <a href="/wiki/9th_Cavalry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="9th Cavalry Regiment (United States)">
        9th Cavalry Regiment
       </a>
       . In March 1898, Washington promised the Secretary of the Navy that war would be answered by "at least ten thousand loyal, brave, strong black men in the south who crave an opportunity to show their loyalty to our land, and would gladly take this method of showing their gratitude for the lives laid down, and the sacrifices made, that Blacks might have their freedom and rights."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
        <a href="#cite_note-140">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1904, the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Spanish_War_Veterans" title="United Spanish War Veterans">
        United Spanish War Veterans
       </a>
       was created from smaller groups of the veterans of the Spanish American War. Today, that organization is defunct, but it left an heir in the Sons of Spanish–American War Veterans, created in 1937 at the 39th National Encampment of the United Spanish War Veterans. According to data from the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Veterans_Affairs" title="United States Department of Veterans Affairs">
        United States Department of Veterans Affairs
       </a>
       , the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_last_living_war_veterans" title="List of last living war veterans">
        last surviving U.S. veteran
       </a>
       of the conflict,
       <a href="/wiki/Nathan_E._Cook" title="Nathan E. Cook">
        Nathan E. Cook
       </a>
       , died on September 10, 1992, at age 106. (If the data is to be believed, Cook, born October 10, 1885, would have been only 12 years old when he served in the war.)
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Veterans_of_Foreign_Wars" title="Veterans of Foreign Wars">
        Veterans of Foreign Wars
       </a>
       of the United States (VFW) was formed in 1914 from the merger of two veterans organizations which both arose in 1899: the American Veterans of Foreign Service and the National Society of the Army of the Philippines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glance_141-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-glance-141">
         [135]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The former was formed for veterans of the Spanish–American War, while the latter was formed for veterans of the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine%E2%80%93American_War" title="Philippine–American War">
        Philippine–American War
       </a>
       . Both organizations were formed in response to the general neglect veterans returning from the war experienced at the hands of the government.
      </p>
      <p>
       To pay the costs of the war, Congress passed an
       <a href="/wiki/Federal_telephone_excise_tax" title="Federal telephone excise tax">
        excise tax on long-distance phone service
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
        <a href="#cite_note-142">
         [136]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At the time, it affected only wealthy Americans who owned telephones. However, the Congress neglected to repeal the tax after the war ended four months later, and the tax remained in place for over 100 years until, on August 1, 2006, it was announced that the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Department of the Treasury">
        U.S. Department of the Treasury
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Internal_Revenue_Service" title="Internal Revenue Service">
        IRS
       </a>
       would no longer collect the tax.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
        <a href="#cite_note-143">
         [137]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar_American_investment_in_Puerto_Rico">
        Postwar American investment in Puerto Rico
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The change in sovereignty of Puerto Rico, like the occupation of Cuba, brought about major changes in both the insular and U.S. economies. Before 1898 the sugar industry in Puerto Rico was in decline for nearly half a century. In the second half of the nineteenth century, technological advances increased the capital requirements to remain competitive in the sugar industry. Agriculture began to shift toward coffee production, which required less capital and land accumulation. However, these trends were reversed with U.S. hegemony. Early U.S. monetary and legal policies made it both harder for local farmers to continue operations and easier for American businesses to accumulate land.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
        <a href="#cite_note-144">
         [138]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This, along with the large capital reserves of American businesses, led to a resurgence in the Puerto Rican sugar industry in the form of large American owned agro-industrial complexes.
      </p>
      <p>
       At the same time, the inclusion of Puerto Rico into the U.S. tariff system as a customs area, effectively treating Puerto Rico as a state with respect to internal or external trade, increased the codependence of the insular and mainland economies and benefitted sugar exports with tariff protection. In 1897 the United States purchased 19.6 percent of Puerto Rico's exports while supplying 18.5 percent of its imports. By 1905 these figures jumped to 84 percent and 85 percent, respectively.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
        <a href="#cite_note-145">
         [139]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, coffee was not protected, as it was not a product of the mainland. At the same time, Cuba and Spain, traditionally the largest importers of Puerto Rican coffee, now subjected Puerto Rico to previously nonexistent import tariffs. These two effects led to a decline in the coffee industry. From 1897 to 1901 coffee went from 65.8 percent of exports to 19.6 percent while sugar went from 21.6 percent to 55 percent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
        <a href="#cite_note-146">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The tariff system also provided a protected market place for Puerto Rican tobacco exports. The tobacco industry went from nearly nonexistent in Puerto Rico to a major part of the country's agricultural sector.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="In_film_and_television">
        In film and television
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The Spanish–American War was the first U.S. war in which the motion picture camera played a role.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
        <a href="#cite_note-147">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">
        Library of Congress
       </a>
       archives contain many films and film clips from the war.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
        <a href="#cite_note-148">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In addition, a few feature films have been made about the war. These include
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/The_Rough_Riders_(film)" title="The Rough Riders (film)">
          The Rough Riders
         </a>
        </i>
        , a 1927 silent film
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/A_Message_to_Garcia_(1936_film)" title="A Message to Garcia (1936 film)">
          A Message to Garcia
         </a>
        </i>
        , 1936
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rough_Riders_(film)" title="Rough Riders (film)">
         <i>
          Rough Riders
         </i>
        </a>
        , a 1997 television
        <a href="/wiki/Miniseries" title="Miniseries">
         miniseries
        </a>
        directed by
        <a href="/wiki/John_Milius" title="John Milius">
         John Milius
        </a>
        , and featuring
        <a href="/wiki/Tom_Berenger" title="Tom Berenger">
         Tom Berenger
        </a>
        (Theodore Roosevelt),
        <a href="/wiki/Gary_Busey" title="Gary Busey">
         Gary Busey
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Wheeler" title="Joseph Wheeler">
         Joseph Wheeler
        </a>
        ),
        <a href="/wiki/Sam_Elliott" title="Sam Elliott">
         Sam Elliott
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Buckey_O%27Neill" title="Buckey O'Neill">
         Buckey O'Neill
        </a>
        ),
        <a href="/wiki/Dale_Dye" title="Dale Dye">
         Dale Dye
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Wood" title="Leonard Wood">
         Leonard Wood
        </a>
        ),
        <a href="/wiki/Brian_Keith" title="Brian Keith">
         Brian Keith
        </a>
        (William McKinley),
        <a href="/wiki/George_Hamilton_(actor)" title="George Hamilton (actor)">
         George Hamilton
        </a>
        (William Randolph Hearst), and
        <a href="/wiki/R._Lee_Ermey" title="R. Lee Ermey">
         R. Lee Ermey
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/John_Hay" title="John Hay">
         John Hay
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         The Spanish–American War: First Intervention
        </i>
        , a 2007
        <a href="/wiki/Docudrama" title="Docudrama">
         docudrama
        </a>
        from
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_(U.S._TV_channel)" title="History (U.S. TV channel)">
         The History Channel
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Baler_(film)" title="Baler (film)">
          Baler
         </a>
        </i>
        , a 2008 film about the
        <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baler" title="Siege of Baler">
         Siege of Baler
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Los_%C3%BAltimos_de_Filipinas" title="Los últimos de Filipinas">
          Los últimos de Filipinas
         </a>
        </i>
        ("The Last Ones of the Philippines"), a 1945 Spanish biographical film directed by
        <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Rom%C3%A1n" title="Antonio Román">
         Antonio Román
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Amigo_(film)" title="Amigo (film)">
          Amigo
         </a>
        </i>
        , 2010
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/1898,_Our_Last_Men_in_the_Philippines" title="1898, Our Last Men in the Philippines">
          1898, Our Last Men in the Philippines
         </a>
        </i>
        , a well-acclaimed 2016 film about the
        <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baler" title="Siege of Baler">
         Siege of Baler
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Military_decorations">
        Military decorations
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spainc.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="190" data-file-width="703" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/79/Spainc.gif/350px-Spainc.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/79/Spainc.gif/525px-Spainc.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/79/Spainc.gif/700px-Spainc.gif 2x" width="350"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spainc.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         U.S. Army "War with Spain" campaign streamer
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">
        United States
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The United States awards and decorations of the Spanish–American War were as follows:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Wartime service and honors
       </dt>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Medal_of_Honor" title="Medal of Honor">
         Medal of Honor
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Specially_Meritorious_Service_Medal" title="Specially Meritorious Service Medal">
         Specially Meritorious Service Medal
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Campaign_Medal" title="Spanish Campaign Medal">
         Spanish Campaign Medal
        </a>
        – upgradeable to include the
        <a href="/wiki/Citation_Star" title="Citation Star">
         Silver Citation Star
        </a>
        to recognize those U.S. Army members who had performed individual acts of heroism.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/West_Indies_Campaign_Medal" title="West Indies Campaign Medal">
         West Indies Campaign Medal
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Sampson_Medal" title="Sampson Medal">
         Sampson Medal
        </a>
        , West Indies service under Admiral
        <a href="/wiki/William_T._Sampson" title="William T. Sampson">
         William T. Sampson
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Dewey_Medal" title="Dewey Medal">
         Dewey Medal
        </a>
        , service during the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Manila_Bay_(1898)" title="Battle of Manila Bay (1898)">
         Battle of Manila Bay
        </a>
        under Admiral
        <a href="/wiki/George_Dewey" title="George Dewey">
         George Dewey
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Spanish_War_Service_Medal" title="Spanish War Service Medal">
         Spanish War Service Medal
        </a>
        , U.S. Army homeland service
       </li>
      </ul>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Postwar occupation service
       </dt>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Army_of_Puerto_Rican_Occupation_Medal" title="Army of Puerto Rican Occupation Medal">
         Army of Puerto Rican Occupation Medal
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Army_of_Cuban_Occupation_Medal" title="Army of Cuban Occupation Medal">
         Army of Cuban Occupation Medal
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_countries">
        Other countries
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">

         </a>

      <p>
       The governments of Spain and Cuba also issued a wide variety of military awards to honor Spanish, Cuban, and Philippine soldiers who had served in the conflict.
      </p>
